<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b2047a9d5066472a935ac9541b4e3c6e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '218d17a1-8580-490c-bedc-ca9e960b7ef6'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                var session_id = '07905F4ED6A10E2E36CF177B036A26D4';

                s.src = url;

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1002/aesr.202300296&amp;type=tocOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3F" title="OpenURL Imperial College London" class="sfxLink visitable" target="_blank">Find at Imperial</a></span></div></div><div class="fixedCoolBar"><nav aria-label="Article navigation and tools" class="coolBar stickybar"><div class="stickybar__wrapper coolBar__wrapper clearfix"><div class="rlist coolBar__zone"><div class="coolBar__section"><a href="javascript:void(0)" data-db-target-for="article" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="article_Pop" role="button" id="article_Ctrl" class="coolBar__ctrl hidden-lg"><i class="icon-abstract"></i><span>About</span></a><div data-db-target-of="article" aria-labelledby="article_Ctrl" id="article_Pop" class="coolBar__drop fixed rlist dropBlock__holder"><div data-target=".article-row-right .tab__nav, .coolBar--download" data-remove="false" data-targetclass="hidden-xs hidden-sm" data-toggle="transplant" data-direction="from" data-transplant="self" article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn figures-tab cloned-with-ids empty"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-multi-figure"></i><h2>Figures</h2></a></li><li role="presentation"    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d109551014" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) as one of the next-generation energy storage devices are gradually used in energy field and entering lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) market. Graphite with low price exhibits excellent Li<sup>+</sup> reversible intercalation properties, which has been widely applied in anodes of LIBs. But it has low capacity for sodium because of its weak chemical bonding with sodium. Here, a defect engineered graphite with low graphitization structure is reported. This graphite demonstrates a defect adsorption and solvated ion intercalation of sodium ions by introducing more active sites and ether electrolytes, effectively improving the storage capacity of sodium. Further experiments and characterization show defects increased after ball milling with surface area increased, and the favorable defects on the interface of graphite are significantly increased. The defect engineered graphite absorbs more sodium-ions and exhibits capacitive characteristics with fast sodiation/desodiation process, leading to an improved capacity storage than the defect-free graphite. Meanwhile, the defect engineered graphite can deliver a capacity of 175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and maintain a good capacity retention of 84% at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 6000 cycles. This work discovers a general methodology to obtain defect engineered graphite, which will provide an experimental strategy to achieve large-scale industrialization for low-cost NIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202300296-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the wide application of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in recent years, large scale electric vehicles (EVs) and scalable grid energy storage have emerged. Because lithium resources are unevenly distributed as well as the price constraints, which promotes us to find a similar electrochemistry alternative.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0001_R_d109551006e306" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0003_R_d109551006e306" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nature">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium resources with abundant contents in the earth have been taken as the most promising “beyond-lithium” energy storage materials. Sodium and lithium belong to the IA group with different periods in the chemical periodic table, so they generally show similar properties in the fields of chemistry and physics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0004_R_d109551006e311" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0006_R_d109551006e311" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup>, lots of anodes with good performance in LIBs exhibit moderate activity in sodium-ion batteries (NIBs), resulting from the difficulty in diffusion process of large Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0007_R_d109551006e321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although LIB have been widely used in EVs, the availability and cost of sodium compared to lithium will push NIBs as the expectation for next energy storage technologies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0008_R_d109551006e326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energ. Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Graphite, one of the carbonaceous, is the most common anode material for LIBs, which meets the requirements of affordable, environmentally friendly, and good capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0009_R_d109551006e333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Sustainable Energy Fuels">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It also has a capacity of storing potassium-ions, eventually forming KC<sub>8</sub> intercalation compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0010_R_d109551006e340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We can therefore deduce that Na<sup>+</sup>, whose radius is between Li<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup>, can be stored in graphite. However, graphite has poor storage capacity for sodium with less than 35 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> because of the instability of Na–C intercalation compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0011_R_d109551006e354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - PNAS">11</a>, <a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0012_R_d109551006e354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - RSC. Adv.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So, the conventional graphite cannot be applied as the effective anode for NIBs. Lots of efforts have been undertaken to realize sodium storage of graphite. Jache et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0013_R_d109551006e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported that graphite can deliver over one hundred capacities in a sodium triﬂate diglyme electrolyte compared with those with a capacity of 20 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in carbonate-based electrolyte. Yoon et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0014_R_d109551006e367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported that solvated sodium ions (Na<sup>+</sup> in linear ether solvents) could intercalate into graphite. The solvated sodium ions could achieve reversible intercalation between graphitic layers if the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital of solvated sodium ion is higher than the Fermi level of graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0015_R_d109551006e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">15</a>, <a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0016_R_d109551006e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, due to the low sodium storage capacities and poor diffusion kinetics for graphite anode, it is necessary to improve the electrochemical performance by introducing structural modification, which can increase the storage sites. To address the challenges, one approach is to introduce defects such as vacancies or functional groups, which can increase the surface area and create more storage sites for sodium-ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0017_R_d109551006e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0018_R_d109551006e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Mater. Adv.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another approach is to modify the crystal structure of graphite by using different synthesis methods or doping with other elements,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0019_R_d109551006e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> such as nitrogen or boron, which can promote charge transfer, thus improving the storage capacities of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0020_R_d109551006e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The effective strategies of constructing defects for graphite anodes are beneficial for improving electrochemical performance. Recent studies have demonstrated that defect engineered graphite anodes can store more sodium ions and enhance the electrochemical performance. It is necessary to develop efficient and economical method to design the defects engineering on graphite to apply in the large-scale industry technologies.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we proposed a scalable manufacturing synthetic strategy to introduce defects in graphite through ball-milling process. The defects in the crystal structure analyzed by characterization technologies are formed on the interface and edge of graphite with the increased micropores. Inspired by the literatures,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0017_R_d109551006e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> defect engineered graphite are verified to defect adsorption and solvated ion intercalation due to the synergism-enhanced kinetics and additional sodium storage sites. The rich-defect graphite shows satisfactory sodium storage abilities in ether-based electrolyte, delivering a sodium storage capacity of 175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. And high reversible capacity reaches to 105 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 80% retention after 6000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The defect engineered graphite demonstrates improved sodium storage abilities in terms of capacity retention compared to defect free graphite, resulting from the increased favorable defects on the interface. Therefore, the rich-defect graphite can absorb more sodium ions due to the increased micropores structure, contributing to the improved capacity and structure integrity. It is believed that the development of defect engineered graphite can be applied in anodes, which will meet the high-performance energy storage for designing advanced NIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202300296-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of defect engineered graphite and defect-free graphite are exhibited in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0001">1a</a>, all diffraction peaks of defect engineered graphite are well matched with standard peak of graphite. The defect engineered graphite displays weaker intensity and expanded peak width, especially the (002) peak, than that of defect-free graphite. The decrease of lattice preferred orientation indicates the graphitic structure is destroyed after ball-milling process. The diffraction peak around 26.5° can be attributed to the (002) plane of the layered structure in graphite, for defect engineered graphite, the (002) peak slightly shifts to a low angle, which illustrates that their interlayer spacing are expanded accompanied by the destruction of structure. Then, Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0001">1b</a>) is conducted to further elucidate the defect density in graphite. The spectrum presents three kinds of peaks at around 1350, 1580, and 2700 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be ascribed to the defect-induced D-bands, in-plane vibrational graphitic G-bands, and D’-bands affected by stacking mode of carbon layers, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0021_R_d109551006e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Solid State Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The half width at half maximum of D and G bands increase, as well as the intensity ratio of D to G peaks (ID/IG) synchronous rises from 0.32 to 1.33 after ball-milling, which further verifies a higher disordered structure of graphite with abundant defects. The red-shift of D’ peak after ball milling indicates that defect engineered graphite is partly exfoliated into flakes along the c-axis.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202300296-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb07129d-88ef-4b0d-ac7b-d98837060172/aesr202300296-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb07129d-88ef-4b0d-ac7b-d98837060172/aesr202300296-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0cadb725-a13b-4ddc-9557-cdebd7866c67/aesr202300296-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb07129d-88ef-4b0d-ac7b-d98837060172/aesr202300296-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202300296-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202300296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of defect engineered graphite and defect-free graphite. b) Raman spectra of defect engineered graphite and defect-free graphite.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) of defect engineered graphite and defect-free graphite are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0002">2</a>. The defect-free graphite consists of few flakes with the lateral size below 3 μm. After ball-milling, the regular shape of graphite is destroyed, forming lots of irregular flakes with transverse dimension between 0.5 and 3 μm. In addition, the defect engineered graphite retained relatively complete graphitization structure. The micropores structure is detected by the nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms measurement for defect engineered graphite. As shown in Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0002">2g</a>, the specific surface area of defect engineered graphite increased from 6.08 to 24.36 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, and the appearance of a hysteresis loop indicated the newly formed pore structure in the defect engineered graphite. The porous distribution date about defect engineered graphite (Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0002">2h</a>) indicates that porous structure gradually forms after ball-milling process. The increased specific surface area and porous structure could increase the charge transfer interface and reduce the ion diffusion barrier, promoting the surface-induced capacitance behavior.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202300296-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f0901e2-a929-4ffd-87dc-41a66c839737/aesr202300296-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f0901e2-a929-4ffd-87dc-41a66c839737/aesr202300296-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6adedf45-c89d-4d25-95f9-cf5c0bbef670/aesr202300296-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f0901e2-a929-4ffd-87dc-41a66c839737/aesr202300296-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202300296-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202300296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) TEM images of defect-free graphite. c) SEM image of defect-free graphite. d,e) TEM images of defect engineered graphite. f) SEM image of defect engineered graphite. g) Nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms of defect engineered graphite. h) The pore size distribution calculated in nitrogen adsorption-desorption measurement.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties for defect engineered graphite anode in ether-based electrolyte were tested by galvanostatic charge/discharge method. Compared to defect-free graphite, defect engineered graphite shows higher sodium storage capacity with the voltage of 0.01–2 V at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0003">3a</a>). The defect engineered graphite displays a gradually stable capacity of 175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 10 cycles with a high coulombic efficiency of 98%, and a capacity retention of 80% for 270 cycles. However, the defect-free graphite demonstrates a relatively low capacity of 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under the same test condition, indicating that the defects contribute more than 31.4% capacity in graphite anode. Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0003">3b</a> exhibits the rate performance for defect engineered graphite, showing a small capacity loss at different specific currents ranging from 0.1 to 15 A g<sup>−1</sup> and reversing to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In detail, the reversible capacities of defect engineered graphite are 175, 140, and 123 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 1, and 15 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The capacity is recovered to 160 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density back to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and maintain a good capacity retention. Moreover, the long cycling performance of defect engineered graphite was tested at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 6000 cycles. As shown in Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0003">3c</a>, defect engineered graphite exhibits remarkable long-term cycling stability with an initial capacity of 125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. After 6000 cycles, it can still deliver a reversible capacity of 105 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 84%, which is equivalent to a capacity decay as low as about 0.0027% per cycle, demonstrating extraordinary cycling stability. As a result, the defect engineered graphite has improved sodium storage performance than the defect-free graphite in ether-based electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202300296-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dc9b0527-d542-4257-ba46-2a7dfaaefc73/aesr202300296-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dc9b0527-d542-4257-ba46-2a7dfaaefc73/aesr202300296-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1699f46c-c3cb-4f77-9d79-deacba54618a/aesr202300296-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dc9b0527-d542-4257-ba46-2a7dfaaefc73/aesr202300296-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202300296-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202300296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performance for defect engineered and defect-free graphite in ether-based electrolyte at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Rate capability for defect engineered graphite. c) Long cycling performance for defect engineered graphite at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In order to investigate the sodium storage behavior, we compared the electrochemical performance between ether-based electrolyte and carbonate electrolyte in defect engineered graphite. According to cyclic voltammetry (CV)&nbsp;curves of the defect engineered graphite in different electrolyte, a dramatical change can be observed below 0.1 V. A pair of sharp redox peaks in ether-based electrolyte is related to the intercalation of solvated sodium ions into graphite layers, which would not happen in carbonate electrolyte. As a result, the defect engineered graphite provides higher sodium capacities in ether-based electrolyte than in carbonate electrolyte. Moreover, in ether-based electrolyte, the test current value of defect engineered graphite during the CV test between 0.2 and 2 V are also higher than that in carbonate electrolyte, and the shape of the CV curves approximate rectangle, implying the strongly capacitive adsorption of sodium ions on the defects of graphite in ether-based electrolyte. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0004">4c,d</a> show the galvanostatic discharge-charge voltage curves of defect engineered graphite in the ether-based electrolyte and carbonate-based electrolyte at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In ether-based electrolyte, defect engineered graphite delivers initial discharge and charge capacities of 367 and 206 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, while in carbonate electrolyte, it shows 144 and 73 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacities. The initial Coulomb efficiency of defect engineered graphite in ether-based and carbonate electrolyte are 51% and 50%, respectively. The capacity loss at first cycle is mainly attributed to the irreversible solid electrolyte interphase generation reaction below 0.5 V. Obviously, all discharge-charge profiles are closely to slope, indicating that the sodium storage behavior of defect engineered graphite in both electrolytes mainly are capacitance controlled. Although sharp peaks appeared in CV curves for defect engineered graphite in ether-based electrolyte, no visible voltage platform were observed in its corresponding charge-discharge curve. Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0004">4e,f</a> show the cycling performance of defect engineered graphite at 0.1 and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> in two electrolytes. The defect engineered graphite exhibits stable cycling performance in both electrolytes, but its capacity in ether-based electrolytes is significantly higher than that in carbonate electrolytes, especially at a current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, nearly three times that of the latter.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202300296-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3c67ede7-8e8a-48a2-a1e6-40908b7497ff/aesr202300296-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3c67ede7-8e8a-48a2-a1e6-40908b7497ff/aesr202300296-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/686b911c-9a0d-4cf0-93d7-cc41316f4255/aesr202300296-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3c67ede7-8e8a-48a2-a1e6-40908b7497ff/aesr202300296-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202300296-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202300296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV curves of defect engineered graphite in a) ether-based electrolyte and b) carbonate-based electrolyte. Discharge/charge curves of defect engineered graphite in c) ether-based electrolyte and d) carbonate-based electrolyte. Cycling performance of defect engineered graphite at e) 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and f) 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In order to further investigate the reaction kinetics of the defect engineered <i><b>graphite,</b></i> CV curves are explored through different sweep rates. In <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0005">5a</a>, CV curves of the defect engineered <i><b>graphite</b></i> electrode, display both intercalation and pseudo-capacitance behaviors in NIBs. A pair of cathode/anode peaks are located near 0.1 V, corresponding to the reversible intercalation of solvated sodium ions into graphitic layers. Another couple peaks are located near 0.38 and 0.9 V related to the adsorption of sodium ions on different types of defects. The low polarization voltage and similar shape of CV curves at various scan rates demonstrate that the defect engineered graphite carries a fast dynamic sodium storage process. According to the relationship between peak current densities (<i>i</i>) and scan rates (<i>v</i>) <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, where <i>b</i>-values reflect the kinetic control steps. Experientially, the value of 0.5 symbolizes the diffusion-controlled behavior, and the slope of 1 stand for a capacitive-controlled process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aesr202300296-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aesr202300296-bib-0022_R_d109551006e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>b</i>-values can be visually converted into slope the log(<i>v</i>)–log(<i>i</i>) plots as shown in Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0005">5b</a>. The <i>b</i>-values of cathodic peaks are 0.94, 0.88, and 0.61 at 0.9, 0.38, and 0.06 V, respectively. The defective adsorption of sodium ions in defect engineered graphite presents capacitive characteristics, contributing to the improved rate performance. Differently, the intercalation process of sodium ions between graphite layers is controlled by diffusion process. Then, the contribution of the capacitive behavior can be further quantified by <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, in which <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> represents a capacitive process and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> indicates a diffusion-controlled process. For defect engineered graphite anode, a dominated contribution of 62.6% is capacitive at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0005">5c</a>). In addition, the capacitive contribution gradually grows by increasing of scan rate, and reaches a maximum value of 95.2% at 12.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#aesr202300296-fig-0005">5d</a>). As a result, it can be confirmed that the sodium storage performance in defect engineered graphite mainly exhibits capacitive characteristics, demonstrating a fast sodiation/desodiation process, which contributes to the enhancement of the rate performance for NIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aesr202300296-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/82608e45-26cf-4d0b-9e42-fb8b9c6b48ce/aesr202300296-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/82608e45-26cf-4d0b-9e42-fb8b9c6b48ce/aesr202300296-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3abd7490-68d0-4eee-abef-2437901332df/aesr202300296-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/82608e45-26cf-4d0b-9e42-fb8b9c6b48ce/aesr202300296-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aesr202300296-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faesr.202300296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of defect engineered graphite at various scan rates. b) The <i>b</i>-values of defect engineered graphite. c) Capacitance contribution of defect engineered graphite at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Capacitance contribution of defect engineered graphite at various scan rates.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202300296-sec-0003-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we find that defect engineered graphite exhibits improved sodium storage performance when its highly graphitized structure is disrupted by a simple ball-milling method. After prolonged ball-milling treatment, the graphite surface is destroyed and abundant defects appeared in the graphitic structure, which increase sodium storage activity of graphite anode in ether-based NIBs. The resulting defect engineered graphite delivers a sodium storage capacity of 175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent stability (84% capacity retention at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 6000 cycles). The capacitive and intercalated sodium storage mechanisms jointly lead to a high capacity of rich-defect graphitic anodes in ether electrolyte for sodium-ion storage. In conclusion, this work facilitates the large-scale development of environmentally friendly and commercial graphite anode of NIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aesr202300296-sec-0004-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0005">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0006">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0007">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aesr202300296-sec-0007-title"> Preparation of the Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>The commercial graphite powder was purchased from Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd. The defect engineered graphite was prepared by ball-milling 1 g graphite powder 50 h at 600 rpm under argon atmosphere.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0008">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aesr202300296-sec-0008-title"> Characterization of the Defect Engineered Graphite</h4>
                  
                  <p>XRD patterns were obtained by a Rigaku X-ray diﬀractometer. SEM images were obtained using a Phenom Pro scanning electron microscope. TEM was conducted by using a JEM-2100F feld-emission high-resolution transmission electron microscopy. Raman spectra were obtained using a Renishaw Invia Qontor Raman spectroscopy with a wavelength of 514 nm. The nitrogen adsorption−desorption isotherms were obtained using an automatic specific surface area and porosity analyzer. The specific surface areas of samples were acquired using the Brunauer−Emmett−Teller (BET) method.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aesr202300296-sec-0009">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aesr202300296-sec-0009-title"> Electrochemical Measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical measurements were evaluated in CR2032 type coin cells. The working electrode was composed of 80 wt% active materials, 10 wt% super P, and 10 wt% poly(vinylidene ﬂuoride) (PVDF) binder. The slurry was coating on copper foil and dried, with a areal mass loading of ≈1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Sodium metal foil was used as the counter electrode. The Whatman GF/D was used as separator. The electrolyte was 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in an ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) mixture with 10% ﬂuoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Another electrolyte was 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in an ether electrolyte. Galvanostatic discharge/charge measurements (0.01−3 V) were carried out with a LAND CT2001A multichannel battery testing system. CV and EIS were recorded on Auto lab electrochemical workstation.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
